in &#39;rye&#39;">r</span><span title="/\xca\x8c/: &#39;u&#39; in &#39;cut&#39;">\xca\x8c</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="/\xc9\x99l/: &#39;le&#39; in &#39;bottle&#39;">\xc9\x99l</span></span>/</a></span></span>; 18 May 1872 &#8211; 2 February 1970) was a British philosopher, <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_logic" title="Mathematical logic">logician</a>, mathematician, historian, writer, <a href="/wiki/Social_critic" class="mw-redirect" title="Social critic">social critic</a>, political activist, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Nobel_laureates" title="List of Nobel laureates">Nobel laureate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-stanford_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stanford-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> At various points in his life, Russell considered himself a <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberal</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialist</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Pacifist" class="mw-redirect" title="Pacifist">pacifist</a>, but he also admitted that he had "never been any of these things, in any profound sense".<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Russell was born in <a href="/wiki/Monmouthshire_(historic)" title="Monmouthshire (historic)">Monmouthshire</a> into one of the most prominent aristocratic families in the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the early 20th century, Russell led the British "revolt against <a href="/wiki/British_idealism" title="British idealism">idealism</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> He is considered one of the founders of <a href="/wiki/Analytic_philosophy" title="Analytic philosophy">analytic philosophy</a> along with his predecessor <a href="/wiki/Gottlob_Frege" title="Gottlob Frege">Gottlob Frege</a>, colleague <a href="/wiki/G._E._Moore" title="G. E. Moore">G.&#160;E. Moore</a> and prot\xc3\xa9g\xc3\xa9 <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Wittgenstein" title="Ludwig Wittgenstein">Ludwig Wittgenstein</a>. He is widely held to be one of the 20th century\'s premier <a href="/wiki/Logician" class="mw-redirect" title="Logician">logicians</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-stanford_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stanford-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> With <a href="/wiki/Alfred_North_Whitehead" title="Alfred North Whitehead">A. N. Whitehead</a> he wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Principia_Mathematica" title="Principia Mathematica">Principia Mathematica</a></i>, an attempt to create a logical basis for mathematics. His philosophical essay "<a href="/wiki/On_Denoting" title="On Denoting">On Denoting</a>" has been considered a "paradigm of philosophy".<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> His work has had a considerable influence on mathematics, <a href="/wiki/Logic" title="Logic">logic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Set_theory" title="Set theory">set theory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguistics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Artificial_intelligence" title="Artificial intelligence">artificial intelligence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science" title="Cognitive science">cognitive science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Computer_science" title="Computer science">computer science</a> (see <a href="/wiki/Type_theory" title="Type theory">type theory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Type_system" title="Type system">type system</a>) and philosophy, especially the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_language" title="Philosophy of language">philosophy of language</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>.\n</p><p>Russell was a prominent <a href="/wiki/Anti-war_movement" title="Anti-war movement">anti-war activist</a> and he championed <a href="/wiki/Anti-imperialism" title="Anti-imperialism">anti-imperialism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Occasionally, he advocated <a href="/wiki/Plan_Totality" title="Plan Totality">preventive nuclear war</a>, before the opportunity provided by the atomic monopoly had passed and "welcomed with enthusiasm" <a href="/wiki/World_government" title="World government">world government</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> He went to prison for his pacifism during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Later, Russell concluded that war against <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> was a necessary "lesser of two evils" and criticized <a href="/wiki/Stalinism" title="Stalinism">Stalinist</a> <a href="/wiki/Totalitarianism" title="Totalitarianism">totalitarianism</a>, attacked the involvement of the United States in the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a> and was an outspoken proponent of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_disarmament" title="Nuclear disarmament">nuclear disarmament</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gallery_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gallery-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> In 1950, Russell was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature" title="Nobel Prize in Literature">Nobel Prize in Literature</a> "in recognition of his varied and significant writings in which he champions humanitarian ideals and <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_thought" title="Freedom of thought">freedom of thought</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Biography"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Biography</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#Early_life_and_background"><span class="toctext">Early career</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#First_World_War"><span class="tocnumber">1.5</span> <span class="toctext">First World War</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#G._H._Hardy_on_the_Trinity_controversy_and_Russell&#39;s_personal_life"><span class="tocnumber">1.6</span> <span class="toctext">G. H. Hardy on the Trinity controversy and Russell\'s personal life</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8"><a href="#Between_the_wars"><span class="tocnumber">1.7</span> <span class="toctext">Between the wars</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#Second_World_War"><span class="tocnumber">1.8</span> <span class="toctext">Second World War</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Later_life"><span class="tocnumber">1.9</span> <span class="toctext">Later life</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#Political_causes"><span class="tocnumber">1.10</span> <span class="toctext">Political causes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Final_years_and_death"><span class="tocnumber">1.11</span> <span class="toctext">Final years and death</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Titles_and_honours_from_birth"><span class="tocnumber">1.12</span> <span class="toctext">Titles and honours from birth</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Views"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Views</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Philosophy"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Philosophy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16"><a href="#Religion"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Religion</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-17"><a href="#Society"><span class="tocnumber">2.3</span> <span class="toctext">Society</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Ancestry"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Ancestry</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#Selected_bibliography"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Selected bibliography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Citations"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Citations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Primary_references"><span class="tocnumber">8.1</span> <span class="toctext">Primary references</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#Secondary_references"><span class="tocnumber">8.2</span> <span class="toctext">Secondary references</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#Books_about_Russell&#39;s_philosophy"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">Books about Russell\'s philosophy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-28"><a href="#Biographical_books"><span class="tocnumber">9.2</span> <span class="toctext">Biographical books</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biography">Biography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bertrand_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Biography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_background">Early life and background</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bertrand_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early life and background">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bertrand_Russell_in_1876.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Bertrand_Russell_in_1876.jpg/170px-Bertrand_Russell_in_1876.jpg" width="170" height="270" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fd/Bertrand_Russell_in_1876.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="252" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bertrand_Russell_in_1876.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Russell as a four-year-old</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pembroke_Lodge,_Richmond_Park.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Pembroke_Lodge%2C_Richmond_Park.jpg/220px-Pembroke_Lodge%2C_Richmond_Park.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Pembroke_Lodge%2C_Richmond_Park.jpg/330px-Pembroke_Lodge%2C_Richmond_Park.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Pembroke_Lodge%2C_Richmond_Park.jpg/440px-Pembroke_Lodge%2C_Richmond_Park.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pembroke_Lodge,_Richmond_Park.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Childhood home, <a href="/wiki/Pembroke_Lodge,_Richmond_Park" title="Pembroke Lodge, Richmond Park">Pembroke Lodge</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Bertrand Russell was born on 18 May 1872 at Ravenscroft, <a href="/wiki/Trellech" title="Trellech">Trellech</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monmouthshire_(historic)" title="Monmouthshire (historic)">Monmouthshire</a>, into an influential and liberal family of the <a href="/wiki/British_aristocracy" class="mw-redirect" title="British aristocracy">British aristocracy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> His parents, <a href="/wiki/John_Russell,_Viscount_Amberley" title="John Russell, Viscount Amberley">Viscount</a> and <a href="/wiki/Katharine_Russell,_Viscountess_Amberley" title="Katharine Russell, Viscountess Amberley">Viscountess Amberley</a>, were radical for their times. Lord Amberley consented to his wife\'s affair with their children\'s tutor, the biologist <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Spalding" title="Douglas Spalding">Douglas Spalding</a>. Both were early advocates of <a href="/wiki/Birth_control" title="Birth control">birth control</a> at a time when this was considered scandalous.<sup id="cite_ref-calicut_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-calicut-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Lord Amberley was an <a href="/wiki/Atheist" class="mw-redirect" title="Atheist">atheist</a> and his atheism was evident when he asked the philosopher <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a> to act as Russell\'s secular godfather.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Mill died the year after Russell\'s birth, but his writings had a great effect on Russell\'s life.\n</p><p>His paternal grandfather, the <a href="/wiki/John_Russell,_1st_Earl_Russell" title="John Russell, 1st Earl Russell">Earl Russell</a>, had been asked twice by <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a> to form a government, serving her as <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Prime Minister of the United Kingdom">Prime Minister</a> in the 1840s and 1860s.<sup id="cite_ref-John_R_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John_R-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> The Russells had been prominent in England for several centuries before this, coming to power and the <a href="/wiki/Peerage" title="Peerage">peerage</a> with the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Tudor_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Tudor dynasty">Tudor dynasty</a> (see: <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Bedford" title="Duke of Bedford">Duke of Bedford</a>). They established themselves as one of the leading British <a href="/wiki/British_Whig_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="British Whig Party">Whig</a> families, and participated in every great political event from the <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Monasteries" title="Dissolution of the Monasteries">Dissolution of the Monasteries</a> in 1536\xe2\x80\x931540 to the <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a> in 1688\xe2\x80\x931689 and the <a href="/wiki/Great_Reform_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Reform Act">Great Reform Act</a> in 1832.<sup id="cite_ref-John_R_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-John_R-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-peerage_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-peerage-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Lady Amberley was the daughter of <a href="/wiki/Edward_Stanley,_2nd_Baron_Stanley_of_Alderley" title="Edward Stanley, 2nd Baron Stanley of Alderley">Lord</a> and <a href="/wiki/Henrietta_Stanley,_Baroness_Stanley_of_Alderley" title="Henrietta Stanley, Baroness Stanley of Alderley">Lady Stanley of Alderley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gallery_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gallery-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Russell often feared the ridicule of his maternal grandmother,<sup id="cite_ref-Booth_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Booth-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> one of the campaigners for <a href="/wiki/Education_of_women" class="mw-redirect" title="Education of women">education of women</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood_and_adolescence">Childhood and adolescence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bertrand_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Childhood and adolescence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Russell had two siblings: brother <a href="/wiki/Frank_Russell,_2nd_Earl_Russell" title="Frank Russell, 2nd Earl Russell">Frank</a> (nearly seven years older than Bertrand), and sister Rachel (four years older). In June 1874 Russell\'s mother died of <a href="/wiki/Diphtheria" title="Diphtheria">diphtheria</a>, followed shortly by Rachel\'s death. In January 1876, his father died of <a href="/wiki/Bronchitis" title="Bronchitis">bronchitis</a> following a long period of <a href="/wiki/Major_depressive_disorder" title="Major depressive disorder">depression</a>. Frank and Bertrand were placed in the care of their staunchly <a href="/wiki/Victorian_morality" title="Victorian morality">Victorian</a> paternal grandparents, who lived at <a href="/wiki/Pembroke_Lodge,_Richmond_Park" title="Pembroke Lodge, Richmond Park">Pembroke Lodge</a> in <a href="/wiki/Richmond_Park" title="Richmond Park">Richmond Park</a>. His grandfather, former Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/John_Russell,_1st_Earl_Russell" title="John Russell, 1st Earl Russell">Earl Russell</a>, died in 1878, and was remembered by Russell as a kindly old man in a wheelchair. His grandmother, the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Frances_Russell,_Countess_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Frances Russell, Countess Russell (page does not exist)">Countess Russell</a> (n\xc3\xa9e Lady Frances Elliot), was the dominant family figure for the rest of Russell\'s childhood and youth.<sup id="cite_ref-Gallery_76-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gallery-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-calicut_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-calicut-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The countess was from a Scottish <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterian">Presbyterian</a> family, and successfully petitioned the <a href="/wiki/Court_of_Chancery" title="Court of Chancery">Court of Chancery</a> to set aside a provision in Amberley\'s will requiring the children to be raised as agnostics. Despite her religious conservatism, she held progressive views in other areas (accepting <a href="/wiki/Darwinism" title="Darwinism">Darwinism</a> and supporting <a href="/wiki/Home_Rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Home Rule">Irish Home Rule</a>), and her influence on Bertrand Russell\'s outlook on <a href="/wiki/Social_justice" title="Social justice">social justice</a> and standing up for principle remained with him throughout his life. (One could challenge the view that Bertrand stood up for his principles, based on his own well-known quotation: "I would never die for my beliefs because I might be wrong".) Her favourite Bible verse, "Thou shalt not follow a multitude to do evil" (<a href="/wiki/Book_of_Exodus" title="Book of Exodus">Exodus</a> 23:2), became his motto. The atmosphere at Pembroke Lodge was one of frequent prayer, emotional repression, and formality; Frank reacted to this with open rebellion, but the young Bertrand learned to hide his feelings.\n</p><p>Russell\'s adolescence was very lonely, and he often contemplated suicide. He remarked in his autobiography that his keenest interests were in religion and mathematics, and that only his wish to know more mathematics kept him from suicide.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> He was educated at home by a series of tutors.<sup id="cite_ref-nobel_prize_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nobel_prize-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> When Russell was eleven years old, his brother Frank introduced him to the work of <a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a>, which transformed his life.<sup id="cite_ref-calicut_80-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-calicut-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Greeks_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greeks-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During these formative years he also discovered the works of <a href="/wiki/Percy_Bysshe_Shelley" title="Percy Bysshe Shelley">Percy Bysshe Shelley</a>. In his autobiography, he writes: "I spent all my spare time reading him, and learning him by heart, knowing no one to whom I could speak of what I thought or felt, I used to reflect how wonderful it would have been to know Shelley, and to wonder whether I should meet any live human being with whom I should feel so much sympathy".<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Russell claimed that beginning at age 15, he spent considerable time thinking about the validity of <a href="/wiki/Dogma#In_religion" title="Dogma">Christian religious dogma</a>, which he found very unconvincing.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> At this age, he came to the conclusion that there is no <a href="/wiki/Free_will" title="Free will">free will</a> and, two years later, that there is no life after death. Finally, at the age of 18, after reading Mill\'s <i>Autobiography</i>, he abandoned the "<a href="/wiki/First_Cause" class="mw-redirect" title="First Cause">First Cause</a>" argument and became an <a href="/wiki/Atheist" class="mw-redirect" title="Atheist">atheist</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>He traveled to the continent in 1890 with an American friend, <a href="/wiki/Edward_FitzGerald_(mountaineer)" title="Edward FitzGerald (mountaineer)">Edward FitzGerald</a>, and with FitzGerald\'s family he visited the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Exhibition_of_1889" class="mw-redirect" title="Paris Exhibition of 1889">Paris Exhibition of 1889</a> and was able to climb the <a href="/wiki/Eiffel_Tower" title="Eiffel Tower">Eiffel Tower</a> soon after it was completed.<sup id="cite_ref-Russell1967a_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Russell1967a-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="University_and_first_marriage">University and first marriage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bertrand_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: University and first marriage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Russell won a scholarship to read for the <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_Tripos" title="Mathematical Tripos">Mathematical Tripos</a> at <a href="/wiki/Trinity_College,_Cambridge" title="Trinity College, Cambridge">Trinity College, Cambridge</a>, and commenced his studies there in 1890,<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> taking as coach <a href="/wiki/Robert_Rumsey_Webb" title="Robert Rumsey Webb">Robert Rumsey Webb</a>. He became acquainted with the younger <a href="/wiki/George_Edward_Moore" class="mw-redirect" title="George Edward Moore">George Edward Moore</a> and came under the influence of <a href="/wiki/Alfred_North_Whitehead" title="Alfred North Whitehead">Alfred North Whitehead</a>, who recommended him to the <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Apostles" title="Cambridge Apostles">Cambridge Apostles</a>. He quickly distinguished himself in mathematics and philosophy, graduating as seventh <a href="/wiki/Wrangler_(University_of_Cambridge)" title="Wrangler (University of Cambridge)">Wrangler</a> in the former in 1893 and becoming a Fellow in the latter in 1895.<sup id="cite_ref-Whitehead_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whitehead-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JSTOR_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JSTOR-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Russell was 17&#160;years old in the summer of 1889 when he met the family of <a href="/wiki/Alys_Pearsall_Smith" title="Alys Pearsall Smith">Alys Pearsall Smith</a>, an American <a href="/wiki/Religious_Society_of_Friends" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious Society of Friends">Quaker</a> five years older, who was a graduate of <a href="/wiki/Bryn_Mawr_College" title="Bryn Mawr College">Bryn Mawr College</a> near <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Russell1967b_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Russell1967b-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Monk1996_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Monk1996-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> He became a friend of the Pearsall Smith family &#8211; they knew him primarily as "Lord John\'s grandson" and enjoyed showing him off.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>He soon fell in love with the puritanical, high-minded Alys, and, contrary to his grandmother\'s wishes, married her on 13&#160;December 1894. Their marriage began to fall apart in 1901 when it occurred to Russell, while he was cycling, that he no longer loved her.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> She asked him if he loved her and he replied that he did not. Russell also disliked Alys\'s mother, finding her controlling and cruel. It was to be a hollow shell of a marriage. A lengthy period of separation began in 1911 with Russell\'s affair with <a href="/wiki/Lady_Ottoline_Morrell" title="Lady Ottoline Morrell">Lady Ottoline Morrell</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-the_Journal_of_the_Bertrand_Russell_Archives_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-the_Journal_of_the_Bertrand_Russell_Archives-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> and he and Alys finally divorced in 1921 to enable Russell to remarry.<sup id="cite_ref-Russell2002_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Russell2002-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During his years of separation from Alys, Russell had passionate (and often simultaneous) affairs with a number of women, including Morrell and the actress <a href="/wiki/Lady_Constance_Malleson" title="Lady Constance Malleson">Lady Constance Malleson</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-private_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-private-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Some have suggested that at this point he had an affair with <a href="/wiki/Vivienne_Haigh-Wood" class="mw-redirect" title="Vivienne Haigh-Wood">Vivienne Haigh-Wood</a>, the English governess and writer, and first wife of <a href="/wiki/T._S._Eliot" title="T. S. Eliot">T. S. Eliot</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_career">Early career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bertrand_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Russell1907-2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Russell1907-2.jpg/170px-Russell1907-2.jpg" width="170" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Russell1907-2.jpg/255px-Russell1907-2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3a/Russell1907-2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="316" data-file-height="414" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Russell1907-2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Russell in 1907</div></div></div>\n<p>Russell began his published work in 1896 with <i>German Social Democracy</i>, a study in politics that was an early indication of a lifelong interest in political and social theory. In 1896 he taught German social democracy at the <a href="/wiki/London_School_of_Economics" title="London School of Economics">London School of Economics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-LSE_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LSE-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> He was a member of the <a href="/wiki/Coefficients_(dining_club)" title="Coefficients (dining club)">Coefficients dining club</a> of social reformers set up in 1902 by the <a href="/wiki/Fabian_Society" title="Fabian Society">Fabian</a> campaigners <a href="/wiki/Sidney_Webb" class="mw-redirect" title="Sidney Webb">Sidney</a> and <a href="/wiki/Beatrice_Webb" title="Beatrice Webb">Beatrice Webb</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-LettersPg16_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LettersPg16-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>He now started an intensive study of the <a href="/wiki/Foundations_of_mathematics" title="Foundations of mathematics">foundations of mathematics</a> at Trinity.\nIn 1898 he wrote <i>An Essay on the Foundations of Geometry</i> which discussed the <a href="/wiki/Cayley%E2%80%93Klein_metric" title="Cayley\xe2\x80\x93Klein metric">Cayley\xe2\x80\x93Klein metrics</a> used for <a href="/wiki/Non-Euclidean_geometry" title="Non-Euclidean geometry">non-Euclidean geometry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\nHe attended the <a href="/wiki/International_Congress_of_Philosophy" class="mw-redirect" title="International Congress of Philosophy">International Congress of Philosophy</a> in Paris in 1900 where he met <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Peano" title="Giuseppe Peano">Giuseppe Peano</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Padoa" title="Alessandro Padoa">Alessandro Padoa</a>.  The Italians had responded to <a href="/wiki/Georg_Cantor" title="Georg Cantor">Georg Cantor</a>, making a science of <a href="/wiki/Set_theory" title="Set theory">set theory</a>; they gave Russell their literature including the <a href="/wiki/Formulario_mathematico" title="Formulario mathematico">Formulario mathematico</a>. Russell was impressed by the precision of Peano\'s arguments at the Congress, read the literature upon returning to England, and came upon <a href="/wiki/Russell%27s_paradox" title="Russell&#39;s paradox">Russell\'s paradox</a>. In 1903 he published <i><a href="/wiki/The_Principles_of_Mathematics" title="The Principles of Mathematics">The Principles of Mathematics</a></i>, a work on foundations of mathematics. It advanced a thesis of <a href="/wiki/Logicism" title="Logicism">logicism</a>, that mathematics and logic are one and the same.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the age of 29, in February 1901, Russell underwent what he called a "sort of mystic illumination", after witnessing <a href="/wiki/Alfred_North_Whitehead" title="Alfred North Whitehead">Whitehead</a>\'s wife\'s acute suffering in an <a href="/wiki/Angina_pectoris" class="mw-redirect" title="Angina pectoris">angina</a> attack. "I found myself filled with semi-mystical feelings about beauty ... and with a desire almost as profound as that of the <a href="/wiki/Buddha" class="mw-redirect" title="Buddha">Buddha</a> to find some philosophy which should make human life endurable", Russell would later recall. "At the end of those five minutes, I had become a completely different person."<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1905 he wrote the essay "<a href="/wiki/On_Denoting" title="On Denoting">On Denoting</a>", which was published in the philosophical journal <i><a href="/wiki/Mind_(journal)" title="Mind (journal)">Mind</a></i>. Russell was elected a <a href="/wiki/List_of_Fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_elected_in_1908" title="List of Fellows of the Royal Society elected in 1908">Fellow of the Royal Society (FRS) in 1908</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-frs_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frs-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gallery_76-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gallery-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The three-volume <i><a href="/wiki/Principia_Mathematica" title="Principia Mathematica">Principia Mathematica</a></i>, written with Whitehead, was published between 1910 and 1913. This, along with the earlier <i>The Principles of Mathematics</i>, soon made Russell world-famous in his field.\n</p><p>In 1910 he became a <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a> lecturer at Trinity College where he studied. He was considered for a Fellowship, which would give him a vote in the college government and protect him from being fired for his opinions, but was passed over because he was "anti-clerical", essentially because he was agnostic. He was approached by the Austrian engineering student <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Wittgenstein" title="Ludwig Wittgenstein">Ludwig Wittgenstein</a>, who became his PhD student. Russell viewed Wittgenstein as a genius and a successor who would continue his work on logic. He spent hours dealing with Wittgenstein\'s various <a href="/wiki/Phobia" title="Phobia">phobias</a> and his frequent bouts of despair. This was often a drain on Russell\'s energy, but Russell continued to be fascinated by him and encouraged his academic development, including the publication of Wittgenstein\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Tractatus_Logico-Philosophicus" title="Tractatus Logico-Philosophicus">Tractatus Logico-Philosophicus</a></i> in 1922.<sup id="cite_ref-Wittgenstein_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wittgenstein-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Russell delivered his lectures on <a href="/wiki/Logical_Atomism" class="mw-redirect" title="Logical Atomism">Logical Atomism</a>, his version of these ideas, in 1918, before the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>. Wittgenstein was, at that time, serving in the Austrian Army and subsequently spent nine months in an Italian <a href="/wiki/Prisoner_of_war" title="Prisoner of war">prisoner of war</a> camp at the end of the conflict.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First_World_War">First World War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bertrand_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: First World War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>During World War I, Russell was one of the few people to engage in active <a href="/wiki/Opposition_to_World_War_I" title="Opposition to World War I">pacifist activities</a> and in 1916, because of his lack of a Fellowship, he was dismissed from Trinity College following his conviction under the <a href="/wiki/Defence_of_the_Realm_Act_1914" title="Defence of the Realm Act 1914">Defence of the Realm Act 1914</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> He later described this as an illegitimate means the state used to violate freedom of expression, in <a href="/wiki/Free_Thought_and_Official_Propaganda" title="Free Thought and Official Propaganda">Free Thought and Official Propaganda</a>. Russell played a significant part in the <i>Leeds Convention</i> in June 1917, a historic event which saw well over a thousand "anti-war socialists" gather; many being delegates from the <a href="/wiki/Independent_Labour_Party" title="Independent Labour Party">Independent Labour Party</a> and the Socialist Party, united in their pacifist beliefs and advocating a peace settlement.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> The international press reported that Russell appeared with a number of Labour <a href="/wiki/Member_of_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of Parliament">MPs</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Ramsay_MacDonald" title="Ramsay MacDonald">Ramsay MacDonald</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philip_Snowden" class="mw-redirect" title="Philip Snowden">Philip Snowden</a>, as well as former <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberal</a> MP and anti-conscription campaigner, Professor <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Lupton" title="Arnold Lupton">Arnold Lupton</a>.  After the event, Russell told <a href="/wiki/Lady_Ottoline_Morrell" title="Lady Ottoline Morrell">Lady Ottoline Morrell</a> that, "to my surprise, when I got up to speak, I was given the greatest ovation that was possible to give anybody".<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Trinity incident resulted in Russell being fined \xc2\xa3100, which he refused to pay in hope that he would be sent to prison, but his books were sold at auction to raise the money. The books were bought by friends; he later treasured his copy of the <a href="/wiki/King_James_Version" title="King James Version">King James Bible</a> that was stamped "Confiscated by Cambridge Police".\n</p><p>A later conviction for publicly lecturing against inviting the US to enter the war on the United Kingdom\'s side resulted in six months\' imprisonment in <a href="/wiki/Brixton_(HM_Prison)" class="mw-redirect" title="Brixton (HM Prison)">Brixton prison</a> (see <i><a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell%27s_views_on_society" class="mw-redirect" title="Bertrand Russell&#39;s views on society">Bertrand Russell\'s views on society</a></i>) in 1918.<sup id="cite_ref-Pacifist_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pacifist-114">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> He later said of his imprisonment:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I found prison in many ways quite agreeable. I had no engagements, no difficult decisions to make, no fear of callers, no interruptions to my work. I read enormously; I wrote a book, "Introduction to Mathematical Philosophy"... and began the work for "Analysis of Mind"<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>Russell was reinstated to Trinity in 1919, resigned in 1920, was Tarner Lecturer 1926 and became a Fellow again in 1944 until 1949.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1924, Bertrand again gained press attention when attending a "banquet" in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a> with well-known campaigners, including <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Lupton" title="Arnold Lupton">Arnold Lupton</a>, who had been a <a href="/wiki/Members_of_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Members of Parliament">Member of Parliament</a> and had also endured imprisonment for "passive resistance to military or naval service".<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="G._H._Hardy_on_the_Trinity_controversy_and_Russell.27s_personal_life"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="G._H._Hardy_on_the_Trinity_controversy_and_Russell\'s_personal_life">G. H. Hardy on the Trinity controversy and Russell\'s personal life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bertrand_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: G. H. Hardy on the Trinity controversy and Russell&#039;s personal life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 1941, <a href="/wiki/G._H._Hardy" title="G. H. Hardy">G. H. Hardy</a> wrote a 61-page pamphlet titled <i>Bertrand Russell and Trinity</i> &#8211; published later as a book by Cambridge University Press with a foreword by <a href="/wiki/C._D._Broad" title="C. D. Broad">C. D. Broad</a> &#8211; in which he gave an authoritative account about Russell\'s 1916 dismissal from Trinity College, explaining that a reconciliation between the college and Russell had later taken place and gave details about Russell\'s personal life. Hardy writes that Russell\'s dismissal had created a scandal since the vast majority of the Fellows of the College opposed the decision. The ensuing pressure from the Fellows induced the Council to reinstate Russell. In January 1920, it was announced that Russell had accepted the reinstatement offer from Trinity and would begin lecturing from October. In July 1920, Russell applied for a one year leave of absence; this was approved. He spent the year giving lectures in China and Japan. In January 1921, it was announced by Trinity that Russell had resigned and his resignation had been accepted. This resignation, Hardy explains, was completely voluntary and was not the result of another altercation.\n</p><p>The reason for the resignation, according to Hardy, was that Russell was going through a tumultuous time in his personal life with a divorce and subsequent remarriage. Russell contemplated asking Trinity for another one-year leave of absence but decided against it, since this would have been an "unusual application" and the situation had the potential to snowball into another controversy. Although Russell did the right thing, in Hardy\'s opinion, the reputation of the College suffered due to Russell\'s resignation since the \xe2\x80\x98world of learning\xe2\x80\x99 knew about Russell\'s altercation with Trinity but not that the rift had healed. In 1925, Russell was asked by the Council of Trinity College to give the <i>Tarner Lectures</i> on the Philosophy of the Sciences; these would later be the basis for one of Russell\'s best received books according to Hardy: <i>The Analysis of Matter</i>, published in 1927.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> In the preface to this pamphlet, Hardy wrote:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I wish to make it plain that Russell himself is not responsible, directly or indirectly, for the writing of the pamphlet ... I wrote it without his knowledge and, when I sent him the typescript and asked for his permission to print it, I suggested that, unless it contained misstatement of fact, he should make no comment on it. He agreed to this ... no word has been changed as the result of any suggestion from him.\n</p></blockquote>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Between_the_wars">Between the wars</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bertrand_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Between the wars">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In August 1920, Russell travelled to <a href="/wiki/Russian_Soviet_Federative_Socialist_Republic" title="Russian Soviet Federative Socialist Republic">Russia</a> as part of an official delegation sent by the British government to investigate the effects of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution_of_1917" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian Revolution of 1917">Russian Revolution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FreeLib_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FreeLib-119">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> He wrote a four-part series of articles, titled "Soviet Russia\xe2\x80\x941920", for the US magazine <i><a href="/wiki/The_Nation" title="The Nation">The Nation</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-nation1_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nation1-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sov1920_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sov1920-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> He met <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Vladimir Lenin</a> and had an hour-long conversation with him. In his autobiography, he mentions that he found Lenin disappointing, sensing an "impish cruelty" in him and comparing him to "an opinionated professor". He cruised down the <a href="/wiki/Volga" class="mw-redirect" title="Volga">Volga</a> on a steamship. His experiences destroyed his previous tentative support for the revolution. He wrote a book <i>The Practice and Theory of Bolshevism</i><sup id="cite_ref-Practice_&amp;_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Practice_&amp;-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> about his experiences on this trip, taken with a group of 24 others from the UK, all of whom came home thinking well of the r\xc3\xa9gime, despite Russell\'s attempts to change their minds. For example, he told them that he heard shots fired in the middle of the night and was sure these were clandestine executions, but the others maintained that it was only cars backfiring.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Russell_with_John_and_Kate.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Russell_with_John_and_Kate.jpg/220px-Russell_with_John_and_Kate.jpg" width="220" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Russell_with_John_and_Kate.jpg/330px-Russell_with_John_and_Kate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d9/Russell_with_John_and_Kate.jpg 2x" data-file-width="422" data-file-height="444" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Russell_with_John_and_Kate.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Russell with his children, <a href="/wiki/John_Conrad_Russell,_4th_Earl_Russell" class="mw-redirect" title="John Conrad Russell, 4th Earl Russell">John</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lady_Katharine_Tait" title="Lady Katharine Tait">Kate</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Russell\'s lover <a href="/wiki/Dora_Russell" title="Dora Russell">Dora Black</a>, a British author, <a href="/wiki/Feminist" class="mw-redirect" title="Feminist">feminist</a> and socialist campaigner, visited Russia independently at the same time; in contrast to his reaction, she was enthusiastic about the revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-Practice_&amp;_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Practice_&amp;-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The following autumn Russell, accompanied by Dora, visited <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Peking</a> (as it was then known in the West) to lecture on philosophy for a year.<sup id="cite_ref-nobel_prize_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nobel_prize-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> He went with optimism and hope, seeing China as <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Republic_of_China#Fight_against_warlordism_and_the_First_United_Front" title="History of the Republic of China">then being</a> on a new path.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> Other scholars present in China at the time included <a href="/wiki/John_Dewey" title="John Dewey">John Dewey</a><sup id="cite_ref-pneumonia_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pneumonia-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Rabindranath_Tagore" title="Rabindranath Tagore">Rabindranath Tagore</a>, the Indian Nobel-laureate poet.<sup id="cite_ref-nobel_prize_87-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nobel_prize-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Before leaving China, Russell became gravely ill with <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_premature_obituaries" title="List of premature obituaries">incorrect reports</a> of his death were published in the Japanese press.<sup id="cite_ref-pneumonia_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pneumonia-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> When the couple visited Japan on their return journey, Dora took on the role of spurning the local press by handing out notices reading "Mr. Bertrand Russell, having died according to the Japanese press, is unable to give interviews to Japanese journalists".<sup id="cite_ref-papers_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-papers-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> Apparently they found this harsh and reacted resentfully.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="&quot;The">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p><p>Dora was six months pregnant when the couple returned to England on 26 August 1921. Russell arranged a hasty divorce from Alys, marrying Dora six days after the divorce was finalised, on 27 September 1921. Russell\'s children with Dora were <a href="/wiki/John_Conrad_Russell,_4th_Earl_Russell" class="mw-redirect" title="John Conrad Russell, 4th Earl Russell">John Conrad Russell, 4th Earl Russell</a>, born on 16 November 1921, and <a href="/wiki/Lady_Katharine_Tait" title="Lady Katharine Tait">Katharine Jane Russell</a> (now Lady Katharine Tait), born on 29 December 1923. Russell supported his family during this time by writing popular books explaining matters of <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, ethics, and education to the layman.\n</p><p>From 1922 to 1927 the Russells divided their time between London and <a href="/wiki/Cornwall" title="Cornwall">Cornwall</a>, spending summers in <a href="/wiki/Porthcurno" title="Porthcurno">Porthcurno</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1922" title="United Kingdom general election, 1922">1922</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1923" title="United Kingdom general election, 1923">1923 general elections</a> Russell stood as a <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour Party</a> candidate in the <a href="/wiki/Chelsea_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Chelsea (UK Parliament constituency)">Chelsea constituency</a>, but only on the basis that he knew he was extremely unlikely to be elected in such a safe Conservative seat, and he was not on either occasion.\n</p><p>Together with Dora, Russell founded the experimental Beacon Hill School in 1927. The school was run from a succession of different locations, including its original premises at the Russells\' residence, Telegraph House, near <a href="/wiki/Harting" title="Harting">Harting</a>, West Sussex. On 8 July 1930 Dora gave birth to her third child Harriet Ruth. After he left the school in 1932, Dora continued it until 1943.<sup id="cite_ref-Beacon_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beacon-128">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dora_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dora-129">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On a tour through the US in 1927 Russell met Barry Fox (later <a href="/wiki/Barry_Stevens_(therapist)" title="Barry Stevens (therapist)">Barry Stevens</a>) who became a well-known <a href="/wiki/Gestalt_therapy" title="Gestalt therapy">Gestalt therapist</a> and writer in later years.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> Russell and Fox developed an intensive relationship. In Fox\'s words: "... for three years we were very close."<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Fox sent her daughter Judith to Beacon Hill School for some time.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> From 1927 to 1932 Russell wrote 34 letters to Fox.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Upon the death of his elder brother Frank, in 1931, Russell became the 3rd Earl Russell.\n</p><p>Russell\'s marriage to Dora grew increasingly tenuous, and it reached a breaking point over her having two children with an American journalist, Griffin Barry.<sup id="cite_ref-Dora_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dora-129">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> They separated in 1932 and finally divorced. On 18 January 1936, Russell married his third wife, an <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">Oxford</a> undergraduate named <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Russell_(nee_Spence)" class="mw-redirect" title="Patricia Russell (nee Spence)">Patricia ("Peter") Spence</a>, who had been his children\'s governess since 1930. Russell and Peter had one son, <a href="/wiki/Conrad_Russell,_5th_Earl_Russell" title="Conrad Russell, 5th Earl Russell">Conrad Sebastian Robert Russell</a>, 5th Earl Russell, who became a prominent historian and one of the leading figures in the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Democrats_(UK)" title="Liberal Democrats (UK)">Liberal Democrat</a> party.<sup id="cite_ref-Gallery_76-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gallery-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Russell returned to the <a href="/wiki/London_School_of_Economics" title="London School of Economics">London School of Economics</a> to lecture on the science of power in 1937.<sup id="cite_ref-LSE_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LSE-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the 1930s, Russell became a close friend and collaborator of <a href="/wiki/V._K._Krishna_Menon" title="V. K. Krishna Menon">V. K. Krishna Menon</a>, then secretary of the India League, the foremost lobby in the United Kingdom for Indian self-rule.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Vagueness" title="Wikipedia:Vagueness"><span title="This information is too vague. (July 2014)">vague</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_World_War">Second World War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bertrand_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Second World War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Russell opposed rearmament against <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>. In 1937 he wrote in a personal letter: "If the Germans succeed in sending an invading army to England we should do best to treat them as visitors, give them quarters and invite the commander and chief to dine with the prime minister."<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> In 1940, he changed his view that avoiding a full-scale world war was more important than defeating Hitler. He concluded that Adolf Hitler taking over all of Europe would be a permanent threat to democracy. In 1943, he adopted a stance toward large-scale warfare: "War was always a great evil, but in some particularly extreme circumstances, it may be the lesser of two evils."<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Before World War II, Russell taught at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Chicago" title="University of Chicago">University of Chicago</a>, later moving on to Los Angeles to lecture at the <a href="/wiki/UCLA_Department_of_Philosophy" title="UCLA Department of Philosophy">UCLA Department of Philosophy</a>. He was appointed professor at the <a href="/wiki/City_College_of_New_York" title="City College of New York">City College of New York</a> (CCNY) in 1940, but after a public outcry the appointment was annulled by a court judgment that pronounced him "morally unfit" to teach at the college due to his opinions, especially those relating to <a href="/wiki/Sexual_morality" class="mw-redirect" title="Sexual morality">sexual morality</a>, detailed in <i><a href="/wiki/Marriage_and_Morals" title="Marriage and Morals">Marriage and Morals</a></i> (1929). The matter was however taken to the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Supreme_Court" title="New York Supreme Court">New York Supreme Court</a> by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Jean_Kay&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Jean Kay (page does not exist)">Jean Kay</a> who was afraid that her daughter would be harmed by the appointment, though her daughter was not a student at CCNY.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Many intellectuals, led by <a href="/wiki/John_Dewey" title="John Dewey">John Dewey</a>, protested at his treatment.<sup id="cite_ref-Denied_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Denied-138">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a>\'s oft-quoted aphorism that "great spirits have always encountered violent opposition from mediocre minds" originated in his open letter, dated 19 March 1940, to <a href="/wiki/Morris_Raphael_Cohen" title="Morris Raphael Cohen">Morris Raphael Cohen</a>, a professor emeritus at CCNY, supporting Russell\'s appointment.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Dewey and <a href="/wiki/Horace_M._Kallen" class="mw-redirect" title="Horace M. Kallen">Horace M. Kallen</a> edited a collection of articles on the CCNY affair in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bertrand_Russell_Case" title="The Bertrand Russell Case">The Bertrand Russell Case</a></i>. Russell soon joined the <a href="/wiki/Barnes_Foundation" title="Barnes Foundation">Barnes Foundation</a>, lecturing to a varied audience on the history of philosophy; these lectures formed the basis of <i><a href="/wiki/A_History_of_Western_Philosophy" title="A History of Western Philosophy">A History of Western Philosophy</a></i>. His relationship with the eccentric <a href="/wiki/Albert_C._Barnes" title="Albert C. Barnes">Albert C. Barnes</a> soon soured, and he returned to the UK in 1944 to rejoin the faculty of Trinity College.<sup id="cite_ref-professor_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-professor-140">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_life">Later life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bertrand_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Later life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Russell participated in many broadcasts over the BBC, particularly <i><a href="/wiki/The_Brains_Trust" title="The Brains Trust">The Brains Trust</a></i> and the <a href="/wiki/BBC_Third_Programme" title="BBC Third Programme">Third Programme</a>, on various topical and philosophical subjects. By this time Russell was world-famous outside academic circles, frequently the subject or author of magazine and newspaper articles, and was called upon to offer opinions on a wide variety of subjects, even mundane ones. En route to one of his lectures in <a href="/wiki/Trondheim" title="Trondheim">Trondheim</a>, Russell was one of 24 survivors (among a total of 43 passengers) of an <a href="/wiki/Bukken_Bruse_disaster" title="Bukken Bruse disaster">aeroplane crash in Hommelvik</a> in October 1948. He said he owed his life to smoking since the people who drowned were in the non-smoking part of the plane.<sup id="cite_ref-letters_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-letters-141">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/A_History_of_Western_Philosophy" title="A History of Western Philosophy">A History of Western Philosophy</a></i> (1945) became a best-seller and provided Russell with a steady income for the remainder of his life.\n</p><p>In 1942 Russell argued in favour of a moderate socialism, capable of overcoming its metaphysical principles, in an inquiry on <a href="/wiki/Dialectical_Materialism" class="mw-redirect" title="Dialectical Materialism">Dialectical Materialism</a>, launched by the Austrian artist and philosopher <a href="/wiki/Wolfgang_Paalen" title="Wolfgang Paalen">Wolfgang Paalen</a> in his journal <i><a href="/wiki/DYN_(magazine)" title="DYN (magazine)">DYN</a></i>, saying, "I think the metaphysics of both <a href="/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel" title="Georg Wilhelm Friedrich Hegel">Hegel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Marx</a> plain nonsense \xe2\x80\x93 Marx\'s claim to be \'science\' is no more justified than <a href="/wiki/Mary_Baker_Eddy" title="Mary Baker Eddy">Mary Baker Eddy</a>\'s. This does not mean that I am opposed to socialism."<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>\nIn 1943, Russell expressed support for <a href="/wiki/Zionism" title="Zionism">Zionism</a>: "I have come gradually to see that, in a dangerous and largely hostile world, it is essential to <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> to have some country which is theirs, some region where they are not suspected aliens, some state which embodies what is distinctive in their culture".<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In a speech in 1948, Russell said that if the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">USSR</a>\'s aggression continued, it would be morally worse to go to war after the USSR possessed an <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">atomic bomb</a> than before it possessed one, because if the USSR had no bomb the West\'s victory would come more swiftly and with fewer casualties than if there were atom bombs on both sides.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> At that time, only the United States possessed an atomic bomb, and the USSR was pursuing an extremely aggressive policy towards the countries in Eastern Europe which were being absorbed into the Soviet Union\'s <a href="/wiki/Sphere_of_influence" title="Sphere of influence">sphere of influence</a>. Many understood Russell\'s comments to mean that Russell approved of a <a href="/wiki/First_strike" class="mw-redirect" title="First strike">first strike</a> in a war with the USSR, including <a href="/wiki/Nigel_Lawson" title="Nigel Lawson">Nigel Lawson</a>, who was present when Russell spoke of such matters. Others, including Griffin, who obtained a transcript of the speech, have argued that he was merely explaining the usefulness of America\'s atomic arsenal in deterring the USSR from continuing its domination of Eastern Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-letters_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-letters-141">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>\nHowever, just after the atomic bombs exploded over <a href="/wiki/Hiroshima" title="Hiroshima">Hiroshima</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nagasaki" title="Nagasaki">Nagasaki</a>, Russell wrote letters, and published articles in newspapers from 1945 to 1948, stating clearly that it was morally justified and better to go to war against the USSR using atomic bombs while the United States possessed them and before the USSR did. In September 1949, one week after the USSR tested its first A-bomb, but before this became known, Russell wrote that USSR would be unable to develop nuclear weapons because following Stalin\'s purges only science based on Marxist principles would be practiced in the Soviet Union.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> After it became known that the USSR carried out <a href="/wiki/List_of_nuclear_weapons_tests_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="List of nuclear weapons tests of the Soviet Union">its nuclear bomb tests</a>, Russell declared his position advocating for the total abolition of atomic weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1948, Russell was invited by the BBC to deliver the inaugural <a href="/wiki/Reith_Lectures" title="Reith Lectures">Reith Lectures</a><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup>\xe2\x80\x94what was to become an annual series of lectures, still broadcast by the BBC. His series of six broadcasts, titled <i>Authority and the Individual</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> explored themes such as the role of individual initiative in the development of a community and the role of state control in a progressive society. Russell continued to write about philosophy. He wrote a foreword to <i>Words and Things</i> by <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Gellner" title="Ernest Gellner">Ernest Gellner</a>, which was highly critical of the later thought of <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Wittgenstein" title="Ludwig Wittgenstein">Ludwig Wittgenstein</a> and of <a href="/wiki/Ordinary_language_philosophy" title="Ordinary language philosophy">ordinary language philosophy</a>. <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_Ryle" title="Gilbert Ryle">Gilbert Ryle</a> refused to have the book reviewed in the philosophical journal <i><a href="/wiki/Mind_(journal)" title="Mind (journal)">Mind</a></i>, which caused Russell to respond via <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times" title="The Times">The Times</a></i>. The result was a month-long correspondence in <i>The Times</i> between the supporters and detractors of ordinary language philosophy, which was only ended when the paper published an editorial critical of both sides but agreeing with the opponents of ordinary language philosophy.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/King%27s_Birthday_Honours" class="mw-redirect" title="King&#39;s Birthday Honours">King\'s Birthday Honours</a> of 9 June 1949, Russell was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Merit_(Commonwealth)" class="mw-redirect" title="Order of Merit (Commonwealth)">Order of Merit</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> and the following year he was awarded the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature" title="Nobel Prize in Literature">Nobel Prize in Literature</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Gallery_76-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gallery-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nobel_prize_87-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nobel_prize-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> When he was given the Order of Merit, <a href="/wiki/George_VI" title="George VI">George VI</a> was affable but slightly embarrassed at decorating a former jailbird, saying, "You have sometimes behaved in a manner that would not do if generally adopted".<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup> Russell merely smiled, but afterwards claimed that the reply "That\'s right, just like your <a href="/wiki/Edward_VIII_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward VIII of the United Kingdom">brother</a>" immediately came to mind. In 1952 Russell was divorced by Spence, with whom he had been very unhappy. Conrad, Russell\'s son by Spence, did not see his father between the time of the divorce and 1968 (at which time his decision to meet his father caused a permanent breach with his mother).\n</p><p>Russell married his fourth wife, <a href="/wiki/Edith_Finch_Russell" title="Edith Finch Russell">Edith Finch</a>, soon after the divorce, on 15 December 1952. They had known each other since 1925, and Edith had taught English at Bryn Mawr College near Philadelphia, sharing a house for 20 years with Russell\'s old friend <a href="/wiki/Lucy_Donnelly" title="Lucy Donnelly">Lucy Donnelly</a>. Edith remained with him until his death, and, by all accounts, their marriage was a happy, close, and loving one. Russell\'s eldest son John suffered from serious <a href="/wiki/Mental_illness" class="mw-redirect" title="Mental illness">mental illness</a>, which was the source of ongoing disputes between Russell and his former wife Dora.\n</p><p>In September 1961, at the age of 89, Russell was jailed for seven days in <a href="/wiki/HM_Prison_Brixton" title="HM Prison Brixton">Brixton Prison</a> for "breach of peace" after taking part in <a href="/wiki/Committee_of_100_(United_Kingdom)#1961" title="Committee of 100 (United Kingdom)">an anti-nuclear demonstration</a> in London. The magistrate offered to exempt him from jail if he pledged himself to "good behaviour", to which Russell replied: "No, I won\'t."<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1962 Russell played a public role in the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Missile_Crisis#Crisis_deepens" title="Cuban Missile Crisis">Cuban Missile Crisis</a>: in an exchange of telegrams with Soviet leader <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a>, Khrushchev assured him that the Soviet government would not be reckless.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> Russell sent this telegram to <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">President Kennedy</a>:\n</p>\n<blockquote><p>YOUR ACTION DESPERATE. THREAT TO HUMAN SURVIVAL. NO CONCEIVABLE JUSTIFICATION. CIVILIZED MAN CONDEMNS IT. WE WILL NOT HAVE MASS MURDER. ULTIMATUM MEANS WAR... END THIS MADNESS.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup></p></blockquote>\n<p>According to historian Peter Knight, after JFK\'s <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy_assassination" class="mw-redirect" title="John F. Kennedy assassination">assassination</a>, Russell, "prompted by the emerging work of the lawyer <a href="/wiki/Mark_Lane_(author)" title="Mark Lane (author)">Mark Lane</a> in the US ... rallied support from other noteworthy and left-leaning compatriots to form a Who Killed Kennedy Committee in June 1964, members of which included <a href="/wiki/Michael_Foot" title="Michael Foot">Michael Foot</a> MP, <a href="/wiki/Caroline_Benn" title="Caroline Benn">Caroline Benn</a>, the publisher <a href="/wiki/Victor_Gollancz" title="Victor Gollancz">Victor Gollancz</a>, the writers <a href="/wiki/John_Arden" title="John Arden">John Arden</a> and <a href="/wiki/J._B._Priestley" title="J. B. Priestley">J. B. Priestley</a>, and the Oxford history professor <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Trevor-Roper" title="Hugh Trevor-Roper">Hugh Trevor-Roper</a>." Russell published a highly critical article weeks before the <a href="/wiki/Warren_Commission" title="Warren Commission">Warren Commission</a> Report was published, setting forth <i>16 Questions on the Assassination</i> and equating the Oswald case with the <a href="/wiki/Dreyfus_affair" title="Dreyfus affair">Dreyfus affair</a> of late 19th-century France, in which the state wrongly convicted an innocent man. Russell also criticised the American press for failing to heed any voices critical of the official version.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_causes">Political causes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Bertrand_Russell&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Political causes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bertrand_Russell_leads_anti-nuclear_march_in_London,_Feb_1961.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Bertrand_Russell_leads_anti-nuclear_march_in_London%2C_Feb_1961.jpg/220px-Bertrand_Russell_leads_anti-nuclear_march_in_London%2C_Feb_1961.jpg" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Bertrand_Russell_leads_anti-nuclear_march_in_London%2C_Feb_1961.jpg/330px-Bertrand_Russell_leads_anti-nuclear_march_in_London%2C_Feb_1961.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Bertrand_Russell_leads_anti-nuclear_march_in_London%2C_Feb_1961.jpg/440px-Bertrand_Russell_leads_anti-nuclear_march_in_London%2C_Feb_1961.jpg 2x" data-file-width="693" data-file-height="476" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bertrand_Russell_leads_anti-nuclear_march_in_London,_Feb_1961.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Russell (centre) alongside his wife Edith, leading a <a href="/wiki/CND" class="mw-redirect" title="CND">CND</a> anti-nuclear march in London, 18 February 1961</div></div></div>\n<p>Bertrand Russell was opposed to war from early on, his opposition to World War I being used as grounds for his dismissal from Trinity College at Cambridge. This incident fused two of his most controversial causes, as he had failed to be granted Fellow status, which would have protected him from firing, because he was not willing to either pretend to be a devout Christian, or at least avoid admitting he was agnostic.\n</p><p>He later described the resolution of these issues as essential to freedom of thought and expression, citing the incident in <a href="/wiki/Free_Thought_and_Official_Propaganda" title="Free Thought and Official Propaganda">Free Thought and Official Propaganda</a>, where he explained that the expression of any idea, even the most obviously "bad", must be protected not only from direct State intervention, but also economic leveraging and other means of being silenced:\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The opinions which are still persecuted strike the majority as so monstrous and immoral that the general principle of toleration cannot be held to apply to them.\n</p><p>But this is exactly the same view as that which made possible the tortures of the Inquisition.<sup id="cite_ref-free_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-free-159">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Russell spent the 1950s and 1960s engaged in political causes primarily related to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_disarmament" title="Nuclear disarmament">nuclear disarmament</a> and opposing the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>. The 1955 <a href="/wiki/Russell%E2%80%93Einstein_Manifesto" title="Russell\xe2\x80\x93Einstein Manifesto">Russell\xe2\x80\x93Einstein Manifesto</a> was a document calling for nuclear disarmament and was signed by eleven of the most prominent nuclear physicists and intellectuals of the time.<sup id="cite_ref-Manifesto_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manifesto-160">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> In 1966\xe2\x80\x931967, Russell worked with <a href="/wiki/Jean-Paul_Sartre" title="Jean-Paul Sartre">Jean-Paul Sartre</a> and many other intellectual figures to form the <a href="/wiki/Russell_Tribunal" title="Russell Tribunal">Russell Vietnam War Crimes Tribunal</a> to investigate the conduct of the United States in Vietnam.  He wrote a great many letters to world leaders during this period.\n</p><p>In 1956, immediately before and during the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">Suez Crisis</a>, Russell expressed his opposition to European imperialism in the Middle East. He viewed the crisis as another reminder of the  pressing need for a more effective mechanism for international governance, and to restrict national sovereignty to places such as the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a> area "where general interest is involved". At the same time the Suez Crisis was taking place, the world was also captivated by the <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_Revolution_of_1956" title="Hungarian Revolution of 1956">Hungarian Revolution</a> and the subsequent crushing of the revolt by intervening Soviet forces. Russell attracted criticism for speaking out fervently against the Suez war while ignoring Soviet repression in Hungary, to which he responded that he did not criticise the Soviets "because there was no need. Most of the so-called Western World was fulminating". Although he later feigned a lack of concern, at the time he was disgusted by the brutal Soviet response, and on 16 November 1956, he expressed approval for a declaration of support for Hungarian scholars which <a href="/wiki/Michael_Polanyi" title="Michael Polanyi">Michael Polanyi</a> had cabled to the Soviet embassy in London twelve days previously, shortly after Soviet troops had already entered Budapest.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In November 1957 Russell wrote an article addressing US President <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a> and Soviet Premier <a href="/wiki/Nikita_Khrushchev" title="Nikita Khrushchev">Nikita Khrushchev</a>, urging a summit to consider "the conditions of co-existence". Khrushchev responded that peace could indeed be served by such a meeting. In January 1958 Russell elaborated his views in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Observer" title="The Observer">The Observer</a></i>, proposing a cessation of all nuclear-weapons production, with the UK taking the first step by unilaterally suspending its own nuclear-weapons program if necessary, and with Germany "freed from all alien armed forces and pledged to neutrality in any conflict between East and West". US Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/John_Foster_Dulles" title="John Foster Dulles">John Foster Dulles</a> replied for Eisenhower. The exchange of letters was published as <i>The Vital Letters of Russell, Khrushchev, and Dulles</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-ref1_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ref1-162">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Russell was asked by <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Republic" title="The New Republic">The New Republic</a></i>, a liberal American magazine, to elaborate his views on world peace. He urged that all nuclear-weapons testing and constant flights by planes armed with nuclear weapons be halted immediately, and negotiations be opened for the destruction of all <a href="/wiki/Thermonuclear_weapon" title="Thermonuclear weapon">hydrogen bombs</a>, with the number of conventional nuclear devices limited to ensure a balance of power. He proposed that Germany be reunified and accept the <a href="/wiki/Oder-Neisse_line" class="mw-redirect" title="Oder-Neisse line">Oder-Neisse line</a> as its border, and that a neutral zone be established in Central Europe, consisting at the minimum of Germany, Poland, <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Czechoslovakia" title="Czechoslovakia">Czechoslovakia</a>, with each of these countries being free of foreign troops and influence, and prohibited from forming alliances with countries outside the zone.
